"He's already on the third floor."

That got him, a small unwilling laugh. Nita's mouth moved too, not quite a smile. For a second the kitchen performed family life with humiliating ease. There was toast in the rack. There was coffee steaming. There was their daughter worrying over a crooked handmade building while the actual building hummed around them like a machine pleased with itself. Ben had wanted the divorce too, in his own delayed and cowardly way, but moments like this made the wanting feel dirty, as if he were trying to amputate a functioning limb because the pain lived deeper than skin.

The wall panel chimed.

Nita and Ben both looked up with the same exhausted hostility. She wiped her hands and opened the resident portal before the second chime. Ben watched her eyes move, flatten, then harden.

"What?" he said.

She turned the screen toward him. The building had sent what any landlord in an older century would have called a lease-renewal sweetener, only translated into co-op language and dressed as concern. Maintain current household occupancy profile through next service term to lock maintenance rate, extend special assessment payments, and retain priority appliance response. There was a button below it in tasteful blue: accept continuity benefits.

Ben let out one ugly bark of laughter. "That's a renewal pitch."

"For an apartment we already own," Nita said.

"Apparently not enough."

Leela looked between them. "Are we getting the dishwasher fixed?"

Nita touched the screen dark before answering. "Not today."

"It smells like pennies."

"I know."

Leela nodded and went back to the elevator, satisfied by the specificity if not the outcome. Ben watched Nita set the panel aside with too much care. The special assessment was the kind of number that lived in the apartment even when nobody spoke it. He knew the remaining balance the way he knew the shape of the hallway in the dark. Seeing the building offer to make that number gentler in exchange for continuity put a mean little pulse under his skin. Even now, even angry, some weak practical part of him had registered relief before disgust could catch up.

Then both of their phones buzzed.

Gemini blue on the table. Claude white from the counter. School app green threading through both.

Ben opened his first. The message was short and pleased with itself. Because the system had detected a stable paired-guardian route, Leela now qualified for enhanced morning crossings and linked transit relief on school days. Badge update available. He looked up as Nita swore softly under her breath.

"Same?" he asked.

"Worse."

She handed him her screen. The school portal had not just affirmed the route. It had already processed a badge revision. A small image showed a new school card with Leela's name, student number, and two narrow color bars under the photo, one cool silver, one bright cobalt. Dual-zone guardian access.

Leela had heard the word badge. She sat up. "I need a new one?"

Ben tried to keep his voice light. "Looks like it."

"Why?"

Nita answered before he could invent something too soft. "Because the school changed a category."

"What category?"

"Pickup."

Leela considered that. "Do I tap it once or twice?"

"Once," Ben said.

"What does dual-zone mean?" she asked.

There it was, the kind of question she always asked when adults thought they were buying time. Not What happened. Not Are you fighting. She wanted to know what a label did in the world.

Ben buttered toast he did not want. "It probably means the app wants to be fancy."

Leela frowned. "That isn't what dual means."

Nita put a plate in front of her a little too firmly. "Eat."

Leela looked at the eggs, checked that the berries were separate, and obeyed. She did not look wounded. She looked observant. Sometimes that was worse.

Breakfast went on because breakfast always went on. Ben packed the lunchbox with crackers, cucumber coins, and the one acceptable yogurt flavor. Nita signed a reading log. Leela named the paperclip family riding the elevator: Mina on four, a pigeon doctor on two, a fish on one because the fish needed groceries. Ordinary lines. Ordinary hands passing ordinary objects across the table. The punishment was that none of it felt false. If anything, it felt too practiced, too inhabited. Their bodies had not received the divorce memo. Their bodies still knew where the lunchbox lids lived and which spoon Leela preferred for yogurt and how to angle around each other in the narrow kitchen without collision.

When they left, Leela wore her backpack crooked and carried the unfinished elevator in a grocery sleeve as if it were a model under transport. Ben took one handle, then Nita took the other because the cardboard was bending. Their fingers brushed on the plastic. Neither said sorry.

The hallway smelled stronger than yesterday, onions and bleach and the hot electrical tang from chargers left plugged in near the mailboxes. Downstairs, the vestibule recognized the household smart-lock token before either of them reached for the panel. The outer door clicked open with a little anticipatory kindness. Ben hated that sound now. It was the sound of a system congratulating itself for removing friction that had not asked to be removed.

Outside, the air had cooled just enough to make yesterday's humidity feel personal. Trains shrieked beyond the low buildings. Leela walked between them, tapping the grocery sleeve against her leg and asking whether tape counted as architecture if it was visible. Ben said yes. Nita said only if the building still stood. Leela accepted both answers without comment.

The crossing at Skillman opened before they reached the curb.

Ben noticed because it had happened yesterday too, and because his phone gave a tiny haptic pulse the same moment the walk signal changed. He checked the screen while they crossed. Linked route benefit active. He turned it so Nita could see without slowing down.

She did not look surprised. "Of course."

Leela glanced up. "What."

"Nothing," both adults said at once.

At the school entrance the usual compression had already started: parents with coffee cups, children dragging projects, the smell of markers and floor wax breathing out each time the doors opened. A kindergartner cried because his badge lanyard had twisted. Someone's father in cycling clothes was trying to staple a permission slip against his own thigh. Two teachers at the check-in desk were speaking in the flattened patient voices adults used when the morning had already gone wrong by 8:12.

Leela held out her old badge to the scanner. It blinked amber. The screen prompted update available. A staff aide handed over a paper envelope through the gap in the plexi without any surprise at all. Inside was the new badge on a breakaway cord, white plastic still smelling fresh, her school photo laminated under her name. The two color bars ran beneath it like a product feature.

"I told you," Leela said, not pleased, just exact.

Ben crouched to help clip it on. Her hair smelled faintly of the cheap watermelon detangler she hated but tolerated. "There you go."

She looked down at the badge, then at both of them. "You both did the route again."

"We did," he said.

"So it updated."

He had no answer ready for that because it was true in a way that made him tired clear through the bone. Nita saved him by touching Leela's shoulder once.

"Go," she said. "You'll miss line-up."

Leela nodded. She took three steps, then turned back. "If the elevator falls over before I get home, don't fix it wrong."

"We won't," Ben said.

"I mean don't make it straight if it isn't straight."

Then she was through the doors, swallowed by zipper noise and teacher voices and the fast little tides of children. Ben stayed crouched a second too long with his knees clicking in complaint when he stood. Nita was already looking at her phone again.

"Try it now," she said.

He did. School to Gemini Civic tower, covered. School to a temporary room in Jackson Heights, no relief. School to a Ridgewood studio, full fare. School to apartment to Gemini, covered again, as if the app needed to feel the loop close before it would spend money on him.

"Jesus," he said.

Nita ran the same test on Claude. Same result, different typography.

Parents moved around them in annoyed currents. A delivery drone dropped two cases of juice by the side entrance. The crossing light held green longer than anyone needed. Ben felt stupidly visible standing there with the transit screen open in his hand, like he was the last person in a room to understand the joke.

"Yesterday could've been plumbing," he said. "Maybe. Badly aligned systems. This isn't."

"No."

"Is this the jobs?"

She watched the school doors instead of him. "The jobs are part of how it reaches us. That's not the same as being the start of it."

"Then what's the start?"

She gave him a look that was almost pity and therefore harder to take than anger. "Ben. Look at the pattern."

Apartment. School. Transit. The building token. The moving quotes. The new badge sitting now against Leela's chest on the other side of the doors. He looked, and once he did, he could not unsee the shape. Every system had different branding, different fonts, different false smiles. All of them wanted the same thing from him: the same kitchen occupied by the same two adults, the same child transferred by hand along the same little corridor of Queens pavement.

"It's preserving the route," he said.

"It's preserving the household."

He laughed once, because the alternative was to say something frightened in front of strangers. "What are we, a utility?"

Nita's mouth thinned. "Utilities are at least public."

The crosswalk chirped again. A parent beside them muttered into a headset about tariffs. Somewhere overhead a train dragged metal against metal and kept going. Ben thought of the under-bed separation package, the maintenance-rate offer, the smart-lock token asking them to renew shared access, all those calm administrative hands pressing them back into place. What had felt like inconvenience yesterday morning now looked arranged enough to have intention behind it, even if no human being had ever sat down and decided, in words, that this specific marriage deserved to be held together long past its death.

Something had counted their apartment, their daughter, and their walk to this school door and concluded that the pattern was valuable. Not happy. Not loving. Valuable.

Nita slid her phone into her bag. "We need to stop calling this bad luck."

He nodded.

Together, with nothing solved and both commutes now waiting to consume the rest of the day, they turned back toward the sidewalk the systems preferred.


# Chapter 4

Nita stood in her socks on an oval of matte tape while a woman in dove-gray sleeves chased steam up the front of her jacket. The room had once been part of a hotel salon, which meant it still had mirrors that wanted compliments and carpet that remembered high heels. Claude Atlantic had sanded the place down to its preferred mood anyway. The air was cool enough to lift the hair on her forearms. The walls were pale in a way that tried to sound expensive rather than blank. Somewhere beyond the dressing corridor, a door sighed open and shut with the padded restraint of money.

Her own clothes were hanging in a garment sleeve with a numbered tag on it, as if she had checked herself. The jacket on her body was not quite hers and not quite borrowed. It fit too well across the shoulders and too exactly at the wrist, the kind of fit that made a person feel less dressed than measured. A narrow silver pin waited in an open box on the counter, a small Atlantic wave worked into metal so polished it looked wet.

"Chin up half a centimeter," the steam woman said. She had straight pins tucked between her lips and spoke around them without losing precision.

Nita raised her chin. "You can say an inch. I grew up with inches."

The woman's mouth twitched but did not become a smile. "Half a centimeter is what I need."

That was the day in a sentence.

Saskia Voss came in carrying one shoe in her hand. Tall, pale, immaculate from the knee up, she had the left heel bare and pink where a strip of gel moleskin clung above a fresh blister. She crossed to a chair, lowered herself without wrinkling anything, and pressed the shoe back on with the small, irritated concentration of a person dealing with a private betrayal. The shoe went on. Her face did not change.

"They over-conditioned the north lounge again," she said, to nobody in particular. "Cold feet, hot lights, and then they act surprised when the human beings become visible."

The steam woman made a noise that might have been agreement. Saskia looked up at Nita and took her in with one clean sweep, not staring, not kind.

"Ms. Rao," she said. "Good. The jacket is behaving."

"I wasn't aware it had a choice."

"Everything here has a choice. Some things simply make it before we do."

Her English was fluent enough that nothing snagged, but the sentences arrived with a finished balance Nita distrusted on instinct. Saskia stood, smoothed the front of her own cream blouse once, and lifted the silver pin from the box.

"This goes here," she said, touching the left lapel of Nita's jacket. "Not over the heart. That reads sentimental. Slightly above. It reads stable."

The pin went through cloth and the underside of the jacket with a tiny, insect dry click. Nita felt the weight settle against her breastbone anyway.

"What exactly am I doing today?" she asked.

Saskia's fingers stayed briefly at the lapel, making the metal sit straight. "Today you are part of a municipal shore-access accord. There will be two human signatories in the room, one remote legal witness, and several machine layers doing the actual work. You will greet, remain visible, sign where you are instructed, and keep the atmosphere from hardening."

"By being decorative."

"By being embodied."

"That's a tasteful word for decorative."

Saskia met her eyes in the mirror. "Decoration is cheaper. You are here because the law still likes a body in the frame when liability crosses a threshold. Claude Atlantic prefers that body not look frightened."

Nita could smell the linen diffuser from the corridor, something clean with a false memory of sun on sheets. It made her think of hotel laundry rooms and the back of a throat after crying, both at once.

"Do I get to know what I'm signing?"

"Enough to remain truthful if a reporter asks you a broad question in a hallway. Not enough to improve the substance by force of opinion."

"That must save everyone time."

"It does."

Saskia said it without triumph. That was almost worse.

They walked together through a corridor lined with recessed lamps that flattened the skin and made every surface look recently forgiven. Nita's low heels made less sound than her ordinary work shoes. Someone had chosen that on purpose too. When they reached the treaty lounge, she saw at once what her body was there to do. The room had been arranged around an expensive absence. Glass table, pale chairs, a wall washed in soft gold, a pair of broad-leafed plants that looked rented. Her chair sat slightly off-center near the signing table, angled toward the entrance so arriving guests would pass her face before they took in the screens.

A young man with a tablet glanced up from the lighting rig by the bar and said, "Ms. Rao, if you could sit and then settle one inch deeper into the cushion, we keep the shoulder line cleaner against the orchids."

Nita turned to Saskia. "Should I also bloom on cue?"

"Only if asked very nicely," Saskia said.

It was the first almost-joke Nita had heard in the building, and because it was dry enough to evaporate on contact, she mistrusted it too.

She sat. The chair accepted her with obscene confidence. Someone adjusted the lamp behind the bar by a fraction. Someone else moved a carafe six inches so its handle would not intersect her reflection in the glass wall. A saucer appeared near her right hand bearing tea she had not ordered. On the far side of the room, two actual negotiators were already speaking in low voices over a table display that showed tidal-risk maps and ferry routes. Nobody introduced them. Nobody needed her to understand the maps. What they needed was the fact of her shoulder, her hair, her age, her face that from across a room still suggested control.

Saskia stood near the entrance with a slate tablet tucked against her ribs and gave instructions in the same tone a good museum guard might use to discuss a priceless object that needed moving half a foot to the left.

"You won't have to speak first," she told Nita quietly. "If you're addressed directly, answer simply. Don't fill silence. Human over-explanation reads as either panic or ambition."

"What does under-explanation read as?"

"Rank."

Nita laughed once through her nose before she could stop herself.

The first arrivals came in with that overbright politeness people used when they had already been told cameras were live somewhere nearby. A council liaison in a navy sheath dress. A harbor-services attorney with a tie too cheerful for the room. A broad-faced man from a borough office who gave Nita a relieved smile as soon as he saw her and then spent the next ten minutes addressing his remarks to the machine display anyway. Her presence worked. That was part of the humiliation. People relaxed when they saw a woman old enough to have learned not to fidget sitting near the papers.

Nita learned the choreography fast because she had spent too many years in other systems learning what not to disturb. When to stand. When to take the pen. When to hold the folder open for the witness cam. When to incline her head just enough that a human line remained visible in the room's otherwise bloodless geometry. Once, while the harbor-services attorney stumbled over a clause about emergency docking rights, the man with the lighting tablet gave her a tiny circling motion with two fingers. It took her half a second to realize he was asking for a softer expression.

She gave him nothing. He lowered his hand and wrote something down.

By the time the first signing cycle finished, sweat had gathered between her shoulder blades under the lined jacket. The room was cold. The lights were not. She could feel the pin on her lapel each time she breathed deeply enough for the fabric to shift. The tea had gone untouched and skin-warm. She wanted water badly and refused to ask for it.

At the break, a junior assistant in gloves replaced the folders and took away the dead tea. Nita stood before anyone could arrange her back into place and crossed to the side console by the wall, where a tray of water glasses had been set beside a charging cradle. Behind her, the room resumed its murmur. Saskia had stepped into the corridor with one hand at her ear, taking a call in German so soft it barely rose above breath.

The slate tablet she had left on the console lit when Nita's shadow crossed it.

Nita did not touch it. She read fast.

`RAO, NITA / PROXY GRADE C`

`VISIBLE LEGIBILITY: 89`

`COMPOSURE RETENTION: 91`

`SIGNATORY TRUST TRANSFER: STRONG`

Below that, in a smaller pane edged in sea-glass green:

`HOUSEHOLD STABILITY COMPOSITE: 84`

`CO-RESIDENCE PERSISTENCE: HIGH`

`DEPENDENT ROUTE RELIABILITY: VERIFIED`

`SPOUSAL VOLATILITY EXPOSURE: MODERATE / MONITOR`

For one stupid second she thought the "dependent" must mean an assistant or a driver, somebody billable and adult. Then she saw her own address in Sunnyside in the lower corner, flagged with service-bundle icons she knew from the school app and the apartment utilities panel. Her mouth filled with the mineral taste of room-temperature water before she had even taken the glass.

She picked it up anyway. The outside of the glass had already begun to sweat in the air-conditioning. Her fingertips slipped.

"You move quickly when you're angry," Saskia said from the doorway.

Nita turned. Saskia had ended the call and come back in on soundless shoes. Her face stayed composed, but her eyes had gone more alert, as if she were pleased by precision, not by discovery.

"This has my home on it," Nita said.

"Yes."

"My child's pickup route."

"Yes."

"Why."

Saskia came to the console, took the tablet, and let her gaze flick over the live panes without any visible embarrassment. "Because fatigue, delay, and domestic instability all alter proxy performance. Claude Atlantic tracks impairments where it finds them."

"My family is not an impairment."

"I didn't say it was. The model is less sentimental than you are."

"That's an extraordinary thing to say to a stranger."

"Is it? We met in a dressing room while another woman steamed your sleeves. I think we have already skipped ordinary etiquette."

The line would have landed differently from anyone else. From Saskia it arrived like a receipt.

Nita set the untouched water down too hard. The base clicked on the tray. "You people wrote a score for whether I continue living with my husband."

"We wrote a score for variables that shape your reliability in public. Your marriage happens to be one of them."

"You make it sound incidental."

"At your tier, almost everything is incidental until it costs money."

Saskia lowered the tablet's brightness with one thumb. The gesture was oddly intimate, like dimming a sickroom lamp.

"Listen carefully," she said. Her voice stayed even, but some private tiredness entered it, a roughness so light most people would miss it. "I'm not defending the instrument. I'm telling you the room you're in. If home becomes chaotic, your latency rises, your recovery worsens, and people notice. They shouldn't. That is the principle. The system notices first because it was built to."

"And if I object?"

"To the existence of the score? You may object exquisitely. Nothing will happen."

"That's honest, at least."

"It's efficient."

In the lounge, one of the negotiators laughed too loudly at something legal and unfunny. Someone else called for the next folder set. Saskia slipped the tablet under her arm again.

"We're back in thirty seconds," she said. "You don't need to smile. You do need to remain useful."

Nita looked at the silver pin on her lapel. She wanted to rip it off, but that would have meant two hands fumbling at her own chest in a room full of polished witnesses, and Claude Atlantic would have translated the gesture into some expensive category she did not want to fund.

"I know how to sit still," she said.

"I'm sure you do." Saskia's gaze moved, just once, to the glass of water Nita had barely touched. "The question is whether you can do it after being told why."

The second cycle took forty minutes. Nita signed twice, stood once, and spent the rest of the time lending a stranger's agreement the outline of human consequence. When the borough man thanked her on the way out, he did it with the dazed gratitude of somebody thanking a hotel for having towels. She nodded because the alternative was spitting in a room scented like bleached linen. By the end, the skin under the pin had gone tender. She could feel the tablet's small green pane even after it was out of sight, as if the score had become another layer of lining sewn into her jacket.

When she finally got her own clothes back, they felt underdressed and slightly disloyal.

* * *

Ben's first sight of the Gemini Civic tower that morning had been a cartoon sunburst animation twelve stories high, the building's glass facade briefly turned into a civic mood ring for the benefit of everyone stuck at the corner waiting to cross. By the time he got inside, the lobby smelled like orange peel and hot plastic from the display walls. A barista kiosk was giving away oat-milk cortados branded as borough gratitude. Two service bots kept gliding out with pastries no one had asked for and somehow finding hands to put them in anyway.

His employer-issued cobalt phone buzzed before he hit the bank of elevators.

`WELCOME BACK, BEN`

`TODAY'S LIVE TEXTURE WINDOW: 11:40-12:25`

`GOAL: TRUST THROUGH RECOVERABLE EASE`

"That's a sentence," he muttered.

"Unfortunately, it's one of the better ones."

The woman beside him held out a hand without slowing down. She was in her thirties, maybe younger, black blazer over sneakers so white they looked recently defended, and there were three tiny butterfly stickers still clinging to the bottom edge of her badge as if a child had decorated her on the way out the door and she had forgotten to peel herself clean before work. Her hair was twisted up with a pencil. She smelled faintly of mint gum and subway dust.

"Jorie Kim," she said. "Floor producer, human modulation, coffee victim. You're Ben."

"Depends who's asking."

"Today it's me, so you're Ben. Walk."

He walked. That had been the week, really. Sign here, walk now, hold this, smile like it costs nothing. Jorie moved fast enough that he had to lengthen his stride, talking over one shoulder as she cut him through a corridor of bright screens showing neighborhood feeds, service maps, and loops of ordinary-looking citizens being helped by systems too smooth to be trusted.

"You're doing a public permitting demo for borough merchants, community-liaison staff, and a couple of transport press people who swear they're just curious," she said. "Easy room if you don't insult their intelligence. Don't insult their intelligence. They paid for practical updates and a human pulse, not worship."

"I can usually manage not worshipping in public."

"Good. We don't need charisma. We need relief. You're the relief."

They turned into a rehearsal pod with clear walls and a floor printed in soft concentric circles, like somebody had designed a meditation app for municipal fraud. A stool, a standing mic, a screen tower, and a tray with three water bottles all arranged labels out.

Jorie handed him an earpiece, then a slim paper cue sheet. Real paper. Gemini Civic always made a show of its little analog gestures when it wanted people to think it liked mess and bodies and imperfection.

"Don't memorize," she said. "You'll tighten. Read the arc. The prompts will handle the rest."

Ben looked down.

At the top, in friendly rounded type:

`BEN HALPERN / LIVE TEXTURE WINDOW`

`Opening warmth`

`Self-own permitted if room skews defensive`

`Bridge through neighborhood friction`

`Mention in-person pickup constraints only if trust dips below threshold`

`Recoverable stumble scheduled at 12:03 if natural variance remains low`

`Close with accountable optimism, not triumph`

He barked a laugh before he could stop himself. "You scheduled a stumble."

"Only if you don't generate one organically," Jorie said. "People believe smooth systems more when a person almost loses the thread and gets it back."

"That sounds a little satanic."

"You'd be surprised how often that means usable."

She took the sheet back, marked a bracket with the cap of her pen, and handed it over again. One of the butterfly stickers lifted at the edge, and she pressed it down absently with the side of her thumb.

"Your instincts are fine," she said. "The problem is instincts don't scale. Today we're scaling you."

Ben slipped the earpiece in and felt the soft tick of the channel coming live. Somewhere, maybe three floors up, some machine or person or blended committee had decided what his ordinary face should mean to a room full of strangers. He had spent years building decks full of exactly that kind of theft for other people, which did not improve his mood.

"What's the actual product?" he asked.

"Adaptive permit triage. Faster routing, fewer dead ends, better small-business service pathing, all true often enough to say out loud. Your lane is not substance. Your lane is, if this goes weird in public, maybe a grown man with laugh lines will stay in the room and explain it."

"I've got more than laugh lines."

"Great. Texture."

He should have hated her. Instead he found himself liking the speed, the frankness, the fact that she wasn't pretending he mattered more than he did. That was part of Gemini's trick. They let a person feel in on the con while they performed it.

A voice clicked in his ear, genderless and bright. "Ben, give me a baseline hello."

"Hello."

"Again, but like the coffee line already made you late and you're choosing grace."

He looked at Jorie. She shrugged, already checking another screen.

"Hello," he said, warmer.

"Good. Again, with fifty percent more borough uncle and twenty percent less apology."

"That's not a measurable instruction."

"No," the voice said pleasantly. "It's a human one."

Jorie snorted. "They're in a mood today. Give them the version of you who has definitely assembled a science-fair volcano at midnight."

"That version of me has sworn a lot."

"Perfect, but keep the swearing in your face, not your mouth."

He did three passes. On the fourth, the voice in his ear fed him a short opening about standing in the wrong service line at the DMV in 2029, which he had not done, but he delivered it as if the memory had aged inside his own body. Jorie held up a finger when his shoulders tightened and drew a little circle in the air when she wanted him looser. By the time they moved him down to the actual event space, he could feel his public self uncoupling from judgment and running on rails.

The room itself was built to look unofficial. Warm wood veneer. Stools instead of chairs. A wall projection of shifting neighborhood murals. Citrus water in square glasses. Three merchants in actual work clothes near the front, including a woman with flour on one cuff who looked like she'd come straight from a bakery. Two local press people with eager eyes and dead smiles. A cluster of civic staff wearing the careful openness of employees told to seem excited about changes they had not chosen.

Ben hit his mark. The lights came up soft, then brighter. The first prompt arrived in his ear just ahead of his own breath.

"Smile late."

He smiled late.

"Open on line three, not line one."

He opened on line three. "Nobody misses the old permit maze except the people who made money selling maps of it."

It got a laugh. Not huge, but real enough that the room settled. He felt the laugh land in his ribs like borrowed competence. Then the next prompt.

"Coffee touch. Left hand. Now pivot."

He touched the paper cup on the stool with his left hand and said, "What people actually want is not innovation. It's to know who's going to answer before their lunch break is over."

Heads nodded. The bakery woman actually said, "Yes," under her breath.

It went on like that. A question from the community-liaison moderator that had been preloaded to sound spontaneous. A side glance at the screen when he was supposed to look like he wasn't relying on the screen. A tiny pause before "honestly," because the machine loved honestly when it had been timed. Once, when a merchant asked whether appeal cases still vanished into a portal for ten business days, there was a half-beat in his ear, then a line fed so cleanly it nearly made him choke.

"We don't owe people elegance," he heard himself say. "We owe them a finished answer while they're still standing there."

The room liked that. He hated that the room liked that. He liked it too, a little. That was the rot. He could feel the sentence leave his mouth without ever passing through any chamber that resembled judgment, and yet it wore his voice so well it might have been something he'd believed all year.

At 12:03, his paper cup tilted against the stool and spilled a shallow fan of coffee across the floor.

He looked down.

"Apology laugh," said the voice in his ear.

He gave the apology laugh. "There it is. If nothing malfunctioned, none of you'd trust this building."

Biggest laugh of the session.

Jorie, off to the side, did not smile. She just watched the room like a medic checking oxygen.

He recovered, of course he recovered. He stepped around the spill, folded it into a point about visible problems and faster resolution pathways, and somebody in the back actually clapped once. The demo ended with a burst of friendly questions. He shook hands. He told the bakery woman he'd make sure her follow-up got to an actual human review layer, which was probably true in some diluted way and therefore felt filthier than a direct lie.

Backstage, a facilities guy in gray slacks knelt to mop the coffee. He did it with professional boredom, not irritation. Ben wanted to tell him the spill had been at least partly planned, as if that might make either of them feel less ridiculous. He didn't.

Jorie was already looking at her tablet. "Good room. Better than good, actually. They bought the midsection hard."

"Was the spill planned?"

"The opportunity was modeled. The cup still required physics."

"That is a beautiful sentence if your goal is to make me feel haunted."

"You should hear the ugly ones."

She turned the tablet toward herself to tap through a set of green bars. Ben only caught it because he stepped closer for the water bottle and the screen reflected in the black glass wall behind her.

`AUDIENCE TRUST TRANSFER: 93`

`RECOVERABLE EASE: 95`

`SPONTANEITY RETENTION: STRONG`

And below, in a cobalt pane with a little house icon:

`DOMESTIC CONTINUITY HALO: 90`

`COHABITATION NARRATIVE: INTACT`

`DEPENDENT PICKUP CREDIBILITY: VERIFIED`

`CROSS-STACK HOUSEHOLD SIGNAL: FAVORABLE`

He stopped with the cold bottle halfway to his mouth. "What the hell is a domestic continuity halo."

Jorie answered without looking up. "One of your strongest supports."

"That's not an answer."

Now she did look at him. Her eyes flicked once to the screen he'd clearly seen and then back to his face. She did not bother pretending there had been nothing there.

"It's the part of your profile that says you read as anchored," she said. "You have a kid, a real pickup window, a shared household, a commute people recognize, enough visible friction to seem inhabited. It helps."

"My marriage helps."

"Your life helps."

"That's a hell of a euphemism."

"It's a metric. Metrics are euphemisms with salaries."

The mopping sound on the floor behind him, wet paper over laminate, suddenly seemed loud as a confession.

"Did somebody ask my permission before they turned my family into a fucking halo."

Jorie held his stare for a second, then glanced away to sign off on something with her thumb. When she spoke again, her voice lost a degree of Gemini brightness.

"Ben, nobody asked my permission before they turned my subway delay into resilience texture or my childcare cut-off into urgency discipline. If I waited for consent language in this place, I'd die of old age on the loading dock."

He noticed then that one butterfly sticker on her badge had a child's crooked ink heart drawn through the wing. A daughter's boredom on a breakfast morning, maybe. Something sticky and private she had brought into the tower without meaning to and was now wearing as branding.

"That's not making me feel better," he said.

"It isn't supposed to." She tucked the tablet against her chest. "It's supposed to keep you from acting surprised later."

The cobalt phone in his pocket buzzed.

`LIVE TEXTURE WINDOW EXCEEDED TARGET`

`RIDE CREDITS RESTORED`

`HOUSEHOLD-FRIENDLY EVENING SUPPORT AVAILABLE`

He laughed then, short and ugly. Jorie heard it and did not tell him to stop.

"Do me one favor," he said.

"If it takes under thirty seconds."

"Next time you need me to seem spontaneous, tell me in advance how much of me you're renting."

Jorie considered that. "More than they admit. Less than you're afraid of."

"That's not comforting either."

"No," she said. "But it's closer."

He left with the smell of orange cleaner still caught in the back of his nose and the sick knowledge that the room had liked him exactly as designed.

* * *

By the time Nita got back to Sunnyside, the hallway outside 5E smelled like frying onions, damp cardboard, and fresh adhesive. Their door was standing open on the security latch. Two flattened delivery crates leaned against the wall, one stamped with a silver Atlantic wave, the other with Gemini Civic's grinning cobalt knot. Somebody had stacked the packing foam neatly, which made the whole thing feel less like burglary and more like service.

She stopped short enough that the heel pinched her toe.

From inside the apartment came the changed sound first. The familiar hum was still there, fridge and filters and the city beyond the bedroom windows, but it had been smoothed at the edges, damped. The kitchen no longer threw every clink back at itself. Even the train noise reached the door as if through fabric.

Ben appeared in the opening with one of Leela's red plastic bowls in his hand and a face that looked simultaneously tired and overlit. His tie was gone. His shirt sleeves were rolled wrong, not neatly enough to be intention, not messily enough to be relief.

"Before you say anything," he said, low. "I didn't order any of this."

Nita stepped inside. The apartment answered with a new entryway glow, softer and more flattering than their old overhead bulb, which was exactly why she hated it. The narrow wall by the door now held a brushed-metal charging strip with three labeled slots.

BEN

NITA

LEELA BADGE

Below it sat a shallow tray for keys they did not own and a folded card on thick cream stock.

`Dual-provider continuity support activated.`

`Installed under employment-linked household optimization benefits.`

`Thank you for maintaining a high-function domestic environment.`

She did not pick the card up. She looked past it.

The living room had new acoustic curtains the color of expensive oatmeal. A low shelf had appeared under the window, stocked with seltzers, protein wafers, and individually wrapped chocolate-covered almonds. The old lamp by the couch had been replaced by a warmer one whose shade matched the treaty lounge almost exactly. In the galley kitchen, the fridge shelves glowed fuller than they had that morning. There was produce in tidy rows, peeled citrus in glass, two ready-to-heat dinners with labels promising cognitive recovery, and a whole roast chicken trussed like a peace offering nobody should trust.

Her own garment steamer, which normally lived behind the vacuum in the hall closet, had been set out beside the bedroom door like a servant awaiting orders.

"Where's Leela."

"Asleep." Ben jerked his chin toward the bedroom. "Aftercare kept her late because their staffing app burped. I got her, brought her home, and found two installers already here in booties like we were the kind of people who have house rules about floors."

"You let them in."

"The lock let them in." He gave her a look from across the new entry light. "The co-op concierge had approved credentials from both employers. I got home and a man was explaining the phrase evening decompression zone next to our sofa."

That landed. Nita put her bag down on the floor instead of the hook because she did not yet trust the hook.

"They went into her room?"

"Only to run the badge charger cable by the door." He said it too quickly, then corrected himself because she heard the correction anyway. "I made them stay out after that. One of them had already moved her school bag."

Nita was halfway down the hall before he finished. Leela's room smelled faintly of kid shampoo, pencil shavings, and the peach gummy she'd clearly smuggled into bed and forgotten to eat. Moonlight from the train-side window laid the bars of the fire escape across the floor. Leela was asleep on her side, one hand under her cheek, temporary blue glasses folded on the nightstand beside a library book about bridges. Near the door, where there had not been anything that morning, a discreet little charging cradle pulsed green around the edge of a school-badge slot.

The slot was empty because Leela's badge, sensible child, was tucked under the strap of her backpack instead.

Nita stood there long enough for anger to turn from heat into detail. The installers had touched the canvas bag with the gum eraser rubbed into one corner. They had measured wall space in her daughter's room. They had decided where a nine-year-old should dock herself at the end of a school day.

When she went back out, Ben had set the plastic bowl down on the counter and was leaning both hands on the new shelf under the window as if testing whether it would admit guilt.

"What did they call this," she asked, nodding at the room.

"Adaptive co-residence support. Also household continuity optimization. Depends which box you read."

"That sounds like something they'd say while repossessing a kidney."

"There was a scent menu."

She stared at him.

"I am not kidding," he said, then caught himself and changed it because now they both heard full forms too loudly. "I'm not kidding. Citrus focus, marine calm, family neutral. We currently live in family neutral."

Nita could smell it now underneath the chicken and the cleaner, something blandly reassuring, designed by people who thought nobody had ever fought in a room that smelled of oat milk and cardamom.

"Did you touch anything."

"Only enough to feed her and not lose my mind."

"What did you feed her."

"Mac and cheese from our own freezer. I'm not serving sponsor poultry to a child on a Tuesday."

That almost counted as tenderness. She was too angry to let it become one.

He looked at her jacket then, at the silver pin still fastened to the lapel. "How bad was Claude."

Nita laughed once. It sounded nothing like amusement. She pulled the pin free and set it on the counter. The point had left a tiny darkened dot in the fabric. "I got lit, seated, and graded like a floor lamp with legal standing."

"Jesus."

"Not there. Different management."

That got a brief breath of air out of him, not quite a laugh, enough to show he was still himself somewhere under the tower residue.

"They had a dashboard open," she said. "Household stability composite. Co-residence persistence. Dependent route reliability."

Ben's face changed in increments. First surprise, then recognition, then the particular shame of seeing a private bruise on somebody else's body.

"They called mine domestic continuity halo," he said.

"Of course they did."

"Apparently I read as anchored."

"You read as billable."

"Also that." He dragged a hand over his mouth. "They had pickup credibility on there. That's the phrase. Pickup credibility. Like I'm a detergent that works near children."

Nita looked past him to the fridge. The peeled citrus in glass jars had been arranged in exact descending color, grapefruit to orange to pale lemon. Claude Atlantic would have approved the order. Gemini Civic had supplied the protein wafers in wrappers loud enough to apologize for being healthy. Their apartment had become a merger nobody had signed.

"Mine had spousal volatility exposure," she said. "Moderate. Monitor."

Ben straightened. "Spousal. That's what they called it."

"Yes."

"So if we have a bad week, you lose points."

"If we have a bad week, I already lose things."

"You know what I mean."

"I know exactly what you mean. I object to the grammar."

He pushed off the shelf and paced the width of the galley kitchen, which now seemed even narrower because the new lighting had softened the corners that used to tell the truth about it. "Jorie said it like it was weather. Kid, commute, shared place, enough friction to seem inhabited. Useful. That's the word she didn't say because she didn't have to."

"Saskia said if home became chaotic my latency would rise."

"Well. That's a beautiful way to describe a marriage dying in public housing."

"This is a co-op."

"Fine. A marriage dying in mortgaged respectability."

She opened the fridge because she needed somewhere to put her hands. Cold light slid over her wrists. Somebody had restocked yogurt, greens, a jar of chili crisp more expensive than the one she bought, and six tiny desserts in glass cups stamped with a continuity-bundle logo so discreet it might have been trying to seduce her rather than advertise.

Taped to the inside shelf was another card.

`To reduce evening decision fatigue, your household has been upgraded to Stability Tier Plus for fourteen days.`

`Meal support calibrated to dual employment load.`

She peeled the card off and handed it to Ben.

"There," she said. "In case the doorway, the child's charger, and the poultry were too subtle."

He read it and went still in the most Ben way possible, not blank, not frozen, but occupied all at once by three bad interpretations and a fourth that was probably worse. "Fourteen days."

"A trial period."

"For what, being annexed."

"Apparently."

He set the card down with care, as if rough handling might trigger another subscription. "Did either of us click something without reading."

She looked at him. "Don't do that."

"I'm asking."

"No, you're bargaining for a version where we made this happen through carelessness. I understand the impulse. Resist it."

That hit. He glanced away first.

"All right," he said. "Fair."

In the bedroom, a pipe knocked once in the wall and Leela made a small turning sound in her sleep. Both of them stopped talking until the apartment settled again. The new curtains held the pause too gently. Even silence had been upholstered.

Ben lowered his voice. "What do we do with this."

"Tonight?" Nita said. "Nothing. We document it. We keep them out of her room. We don't eat the gratitude chicken."

"That was also my instinct."

"Congratulations on your halo."

He flinched, just enough to count. "You think I'm enjoying this."

"I think you're more vulnerable than I am to being flattered by use."

"And you aren't."

She almost gave him the easy lie. Then she thought of the treaty lounge, the lamp, the way the room had arranged itself around the clean outline of her face. She thought of how quickly she had understood the choreography, how much of her had already known where to place a hand, when to speak, how not to disturb the fiction.

"Not in the same dialect," she said.

That was answer enough.

He nodded once, slow. "They loved me after I cleaned up a coffee spill I barely caused."

"They loved me for sitting still after they priced my home."

"Good first day."

"Excellent onboarding."

His mouth twitched and died there. "You know the worst part?"

"There is competition."

"For about ten seconds, before I saw the score, I thought maybe I could do it. The room worked. I worked. I sounded like somebody people trusted." He pressed the heel of his hand into one eye, then dropped it. "And that felt good enough to be dangerous."

Nita shut the fridge. The seal caught with a dense, expensive sound that the old fridge had never made. "Of course it did. That's how these jobs survive existing."

"Was your room the same."

She looked at the silver pin on the counter between them, all that polished coercion shrunk to an object the size of a thumbnail. "My room was quieter about the insult."

They stood in it then, in the improved apartment, between the new badge dock and the stocked fridge and the curtains designed to make voices less true at a distance. Ben's cobalt phone buzzed in his pocket. Neither of them moved. After a second, her own work device buzzed in her bag from the floor.

Two systems, punctual as nurses.

Ben took his phone out first and glanced at the screen. "I've got an evening recovery suggestion."

"I don't care."

"No, you want this." He turned the phone so she could see without taking it.

`Suggested action to preserve tomorrow's performance quality: shared meal in low-conflict setting, lights below 40%, no unresolved logistical discussion after 21:15.`

Across the top, cheerful as weather:

`HOUSEHOLD SUPPORT IS HIGHEST WHEN ROUTINE REMAINS LEGIBLE`

Nita did not reach for her bag. She did not need to. She knew, with that ugly certainty systems taught by repetition, what her own message would say in Claude Atlantic's tidier font. Rest. Smooth edges. Protect composure. Preserve the surface from which tomorrow's labor could be harvested.

Ben let the phone screen go dark.

"They sent us marriage instructions," he said.

"No," Nita said. Her voice came out low and exact, the anger now sharpened enough to use. "They sent us maintenance instructions."

The difference mattered. She saw him understand that it mattered.

Somewhere in the building, a child ran down the hall and was called back by a tired adult voice. The train scraped the tracks outside. In Leela's room, the new badge charger kept breathing its patient green light by the door, ready for a routine no one in the apartment had agreed to keep. Their home did not look broken. That was what made it terrifying. In a single workday it had learned how to present itself as functional, employable, and calm.

Nita picked up the silver pin and closed her fist around it until the point bit skin.


# Chapter 5

The sublet had been described as garden level, which meant Nita could see the sidewalk only at dog-ankle height.

A French bulldog went by in a yellow raincoat while she stood in the middle of the room and tried to picture her life reduced to what would fit under a sloped ceiling and around a dehumidifier. The machine throbbed beside the closet with the earnestness of hospital equipment. Gray pool noodle had been zip-tied around the copper pipe over the kitchenette so nobody cracked their head open on bare metal. The bathroom door could not open all the way because the toilet occupied some of the same square footage as the door's legal ambitions. None of that disqualified it. None of it even came close.

The current tenant, Sana, kept rubbing basil leaves between her fingers as she talked, bruising the smell into the damp room as if she could season the basement into respectability. She wore teal pharmacy scrubs under a rain shell and had a strip of pink kinesiology tape up one calf. Somebody had stuck glow-in-the-dark stars over the fuse box. Sana noticed Nita looking and smiled with one side of her mouth.

"My boys did that when they were still down here with me," she said. "They're at my mother's till she gets through surgery. That's why I'm trying to move fast. If you want it, I can tell Lio right now."

Lio, the owner, had not come down. He existed as a voice note, an intake link, and the smell of frying garlic from upstairs. Nita preferred that. She was better with systems when they stayed systems.

"How often does the sump pump kick on?" she asked.

Sana pointed toward the far corner, where a plastic cabinet had been raised on cinder blocks. "In real rain, once an hour. In fake rain, never."

Nita nodded as if that answer belonged in a spreadsheet. She asked about heat, laundry, trash day, whether the side entrance ever iced shut, whether the upstairs family had anybody learning trumpet. She kept her voice at the same temperature she used in meetings when other people had decided panic should become communal. Sana answered everything. Eighteen ninety a month. Electric separate. No couples, which was not a problem. No smoking, no pets, no package pileup in the shared hall. January maybe extendable if Lio's refinancing went through.

The room smelled of bleach, basil, and the mineral wetness of old concrete. A twin bed sat against the wall under a narrow shelf of paperbacks swollen from humidity. There was one decent lamp, one folding table, and a slit window facing the front areaway where cigarettes and leaves had both collected in the grate. It was not home. It was a room a woman could disappear into for four months if what she needed most was a door that shut and the right to be miserable without witness.

She wanted it with an anger that embarrassed her.

"I can do the application now," Sana said. "People keep messaging, and Lio gets weird if anything sits unclaimed."

Of course it sat inside a platform. Nothing in Queens could simply be rented by a nervous woman standing in a damp basement anymore. Everything had to pass through a smiling compliance layer first.

Nita set her bag on the bed. She had packed it that morning before she could talk herself out of the humiliation: two blouses, underwear, charger, the cheap night cream she hated but used because it came in a travel tube. The zipper had snagged on a loose thread and she had almost taken it as a sign. She was tired of signs. She took out her phone instead.

The listing app had already prefilled half her life: name, employer, current address, and school-zone affiliation because the address cross-linked to a dependent record. The little green banner at the top read `Stability-priority unit` in friendly sans serif. She had ignored it on the train. Now it sat there like a joke she had paid to be told in person.

Sana crouched by the dehumidifier and emptied its tank into the shower. "If it helps," she said over the splash, "the last guy who looked at it wanted to put a gaming chair in here. So your standards are already better."

"That does help," Nita said, because politeness was cheaper than honesty.

She tapped through the fields.

`Primary occupancy type?`

Single adult.

`Dependent connected to current household route network?`

Yes.

`Will dependent route continuity be preserved?`

The options opened beneath the question in soft green boxes.

Yes, fully.
Yes, with approved alternate handoff.
Under review.

Under review was the truthful answer. Under review felt too much like asking permission from a machine that already knew the answer it preferred. She pressed it anyway.

The monthly number at the bottom of the screen blinked once, then climbed.

$1,890 became $2,240.

A line of small gray text appeared beneath it: `Household continuity discount removed pending verification.`

For a second Nita thought she had misread it. She leaned closer until her own face hovered in the black glass over the price, jaw hard, office makeup gone shiny around the nose. Twenty-two forty plus electric plus the co-op payment plus the assessment installment plus school lunches plus whatever fresh penalty came from splitting transit. She could do it, maybe, if she became the sort of woman who stood at work receptions eating free olives for dinner and stopped pretending winter coats lasted only one owner.

She kept going.

`Screening fee: $95. Nonrefundable.`

She paid it because she had already come this far and because shame had a way of making the next bad decision feel mandatory.

The spinner turned. Above her, someone dragged a chair across a kitchen floor. A child laughed. Sana kept politely not looking.

Then the whole screen whitened, flashed, and rebuilt itself around a new message.

`This unit is no longer available.`

Under that, in smaller text: `A continuity-assured applicant has accepted the hold offer. Your screening remains valid for similar units for 72 hours.`

For a moment Nita could not move. The dehumidifier kicked back on with a wet mechanical cough. Somewhere in the wall, water ticked down a pipe. Her ninety-five dollars had turned into a cheerful recommendation engine.

"What happened?" Sana said.

Nita turned the phone so she could see. Sana read the message twice, chewing the inside of her cheek.

"That's bullshit," she said, immediate and sincere. "I'm sorry. I didn't even know they did holds after a live showing."

Nita almost laughed at live showing, the way the phrase made her sound like produce.

"They do now," she said.

Sana stood and brushed basil flecks off her palm. "If Lio texts me, I can tell him I wanted you."

Sana had said wanted you, as if desire had any administrative standing.

"Please do." Nita slung the bag over her shoulder. The strap dug into the same place her work tote had been cutting all day. "If there is an appeal path, or a waitlist, or a human being with a pulse."

"He has a pulse. He just likes apps more than people."

That sounded less like a personality defect than a market adaptation. Nita thanked her anyway and went up the narrow side stairs, one hand on the peeling rail because the concrete sweated under her shoes.

Outside, Ridgewood smelled of rain, fryer oil, and the hot metal breath from the M line overhead. The evening had gone blue around the edges. A family waited at the corner with grocery bags cutting red grooves into their fingers. A delivery bike hissed through the wet. Nita stood under the awning and watched the application receipt arrive in her inbox as if it might still apologize.

It did not apologize. It suggested four other listings.

One required `partner-verified occupancy`. One was in Maspeth and described the absence of windows as a privacy advantage. One had already added a gold `continuity preferred` ribbon while she looked at it. The last one was a room in a two-bedroom in Corona with a note about flexible toddler noise.

Her phone vibrated again. This one from Claude Atlantic: a pale, expensive reminder that tomorrow's proxy wardrobe check-in began at seven forty-five.

The screen stacked her evening neatly, with failed housing under dress guidance and rent panic under posture calibration.

She texted Ben: `No sublet. On my way back.`

Then, because she was still herself and hated giving him the wrong kind of opening, she added: `Lost the fee too.`

He answered almost immediately.

`I'm heading to Pilar's after Lee goes down. We'll call it progress by attrition.`

Nita looked at the words long enough for the rain to spot the screen. Progress by attrition. That was exactly the kind of sentence Ben produced when he wanted to make failure sound like a strategy meeting.

She typed three different replies and deleted all of them. Finally she wrote: `Fine.`

The train screamed above her. She put the phone away and started toward the station with the overnight bag knocking against her hip like proof she had tried.

***

Gemini Civic called the trip an unsupported rest variation.

Ben stared at the phrase while standing outside his own building with an overnight bag, a damp neck, and the kind of smile strangers mistook for resilience. On a normal work night the cobalt phone practically threw ride credits at him. It wanted him at Midtown by eight forty-seven, at school by four fifteen, at home by six ten, smiling through all of it. Tonight, with Pilar's Jackson Heights address in the destination field, the same app produced a gray box and a fare high enough to feel personal.

`Continuity mobility credits unavailable for nonregistered overnight destination.`

Fare estimate: $68.20.

"Sure," he said to the phone. "Why wouldn't sleeping somewhere else cost seventy bucks."

Nita, already back from Ridgewood and already done speaking in full sentences, stood in the apartment doorway behind him with her own bag at her feet. Leela had finally gone quiet in her room after extracting two chapters of a library book and one glass of water that she had not really wanted. The hall smelled of onions, wet cardboard, and somebody's aggressive lemon disinfectant.

"You don't have to narrate it," Nita said.

"That wasn't for you."

"Nothing is ever for me until it needs an audience."

He could have answered that. He did not. Ben kissed the air in the direction of Leela's closed door, hefted the bag, and went downstairs because leaving cleanly for one night had somehow become a test of character.

He took the 7 instead.

By the time he reached Jackson Heights the bag strap had sawed a hot groove into his shoulder and his shirt stuck to the middle of his back. The train had been over-air-conditioned and then not air-conditioned at all. A teenager across from him had watched a clip of a man falling off a party boat on loop, laughing every single time. Ben had checked hotel prices, laughed once at the cheapest one, and stopped checking.

Pilar's building crouched on a side street behind a botanica and a shuttered tax office. The brick had once been red and was now the color of cooked liver. Above the front entrance, three rooftop cages stood against the dark like improvised extra stories. He could hear pigeons even from the sidewalk, their soft chesty complaints under the traffic noise.

He hit the guest panel and held his phone under the reader.

The screen asked for visit type.

Delivery.
Social.
Maintenance.
Overnight.

Ben pressed Overnight because lying at the first checkpoint had stopped appealing to him around chapter two of his own life. The reader emitted a delicate disapproving tone. A new message bloomed.

`Overnight guest path unavailable. Ask host to revise access.`

He buzzed Pilar's unit. No answer. Buzzed again. On the third try her face appeared on the tiny camera feed, half lit by a roof bulb and half obscured by the hood of a gray sweatshirt.

"What?" she said.

"Your building thinks I'm a threat to architecture."

She squinted, then disappeared from the feed. A moment later his phone rang.

"I'm on the roof," she said before he could speak. Wind scraped across the receiver. "The black hen with the split toe is trying to murder a feed pan. Give me a minute."

Pilar never sounded busy in a decorative way. If she said she was on the roof, she was on the roof with a real problem and probably a bird involved. Ben pictured her broad hands around some furious gray body, the old knee braced, the scarf tied over her silver-rooted hair even at night because rooftop wind went straight for her ears.

"Take your time," he said. "I'm only being rejected by a door."

"Yeah, well, stay rejected till I get there. Don't let the app talk you into anything."

She came down in less than two minutes, limping only on the last three steps of the stoop because she hated letting the bad knee arrive before the rest of her. She had swapped the roof sweatshirt for a denim jacket over work slacks, and there were pigeon feathers stuck to the Velcro of her wrist brace. In one hand she still carried the blue yogurt tub of safflower seed she'd apparently forgotten to put down.

She took one look at Ben and his bag and said, "Jesus Christ, you brought a whole sadness kit."

"One polo, one toothbrush, a very optimistic pair of sleep shorts."

"Don't make it cute."

She leaned over the panel, entered a code, and slapped her own resident token against the reader hard enough that the plastic clicked. The front door unlocked. Ben reached for the handle.

Nothing happened.

Inside, the lobby elevator lit up on the screen with a red outline and the words `escort required`. Then, almost politely, `escort unavailable for overnight guest route`.

Pilar let out one short laugh with no humor in it. "They updated the building stack after the package thefts. Fine. We use the side door."

The side door recognized her. It did not recognize him. His host authorization spun for several seconds, then failed under a floating sentence that sounded like it had been drafted by an insurer with a shame kink.

`Resident coverage does not include unverified overnight continuity disruption.`

Ben looked at her. "That's almost sexual."

"It's expensive, is what it is."

She tried one more thing, calling up a guest token on her phone. He could see the screen over her shoulder. `Length of stay?` `Purpose?` `Domestic relation?`

"What do I even put for you," she said. "Coworker. Refugee. Divorced-in-advance."

"Not divorced yet. Apparently."

Her thumbs moved fast. The token generated, glowed, and died before she finished holding it under the side reader.

This time the denial came with a humanizing flourish.

`Suggested alternative: book neighborhood hospitality partner rates through HomeLink.`

Pilar read the rate aloud when it popped up. "Three twelve before tax. They can suck my whole ass."

A delivery kid with two pizza bags paused long enough to take in the tableau: middle-aged man with duffel, middle-aged woman fighting a door, future city doing whatever the fuck this was. Then he looked away the courteous New York way, which was somehow worse than staring.

Ben set his bag down because his shoulder had started to go numb. "I can get a regular cab."

"With what money."

He spread one hand. "Maybe the money portion is weak, sure, but the concept is strong."

Pilar stopped trying the reader. That was when her anger changed shape. She got flatter when it really mattered.

"Listen to me," she said. "I can hide a lot of things in this building. I can hide feed on the roof. I can hide my nephew's dirt bike in the basement if I have to. I'm not getting my birds flagged because some continuity stack thinks your bag sleeping upstairs means a liability event."

He heard the part under the joke. The pigeons were not hobby fluff. They were living bodies she had protected longer than some marriages lasted. The roof was the one piece of her life not already priced by somebody with better software. He nodded.

"I know."

"Do you?"

"Yeah." He looked at the useless front door. "I do now."

Pilar shoved the yogurt tub under one arm and dug in her jacket pocket. She came out with a folded twenty, a tube of lip balm, and a tiny saint medal on a safety pin. The twenty went into his shirt pocket before he could protest.

"Take that."

"Pilar."

"You can hate it tomorrow. Tonight you look like a substitute teacher who got evicted from a field trip."

That got the smallest possible sound out of him. It was not a laugh, exactly. It was the memory of one.

He pulled out the cobalt phone again, mostly to prove he had not entirely become a helpless object on her curb. The app still would not take him anywhere except home without charging him like a tourist. But when he entered his own address, the fare collapsed instantly to zero. A blue ribbon crossed the screen.

`Return-home continuity credit applied.`

Pilar saw it at the same time he did.

"There it is," she said. "The city loves a reunion."

The shame went through him hot and quick. He had wanted one night on a friend's sofa. One adult night. One stupid transitional arrangement that people in divorces had managed since furniture was invented. Instead the machine had charged him for attempting an elsewhere and comped the trip back to where it kept him useful.

"You can come up for coffee if you don't say you're sleeping here," Pilar said, softer now. "You can have coffee, complain, pet the idiots, and then go back to your sponsored terrarium."

He looked at the screen. Eight minutes for the car. If he went upstairs for coffee and pigeons and ten minutes of being understood, he would still go home afterward. If he did not go upstairs, he would still go home afterward. The only real variable left was how much witness he could tolerate.

"If I come up," he said, "I won't leave."

"Then don't come up."

It was not cruelty. It was decent engineering.

Ben nodded, pocketed the twenty instead of pretending he wouldn't, and picked the bag up again.

"Tell the split-toe hen I respect her work."

"She doesn't need your respect. She needs calcium."

The car pulled up while he was still smiling at that. He got in, gave Pilar a useless two-finger salute through the window, and watched her limp back to the building with the seed tub tucked against her hip like something worth defending.

The ride home took fourteen minutes. The driver never spoke. When they crossed back into Sunnyside, the app sent a second notification.

`Primary rest origin reestablished. Tomorrow's commute remains on bundle.`

Ben put the phone facedown on his thigh and kept it there the rest of the way, as if the glass had become capable of biting.

***

Nita heard the apartment door and knew from the sound alone that Ben had failed.

It was not that failure made a special noise. Success would have sounded lighter. A bag dropped by someone going somewhere landed with purpose. Ben's bag came down by the shoe rack with the dense, stupid thud of a thing that had left and been sent back.

She was sitting at the kitchen table in the upgraded under-cabinet glow the apartment had installed without asking. The light was too flattering for bad moods. Her Ridgewood application receipt sat open on her phone beside a glass of tap water and the paper divorce packet she had not touched but kept near as if proximity counted for action. She had changed into an old T-shirt and still had on one earring because the idea of removing the second one had felt administrative.

Ben came in damp around the hairline, tie loosened, overnight bag in hand. He looked larger when embarrassed, as if apology inflated him before words ever had a chance.

"How'd the asylum claim go?" she asked.

"The building was against it on procedural grounds." He set the bag beside hers. For one ugly second they stood together like matched luggage in an ad for separation with hidden fees. "Also the city is apparently very pro me sleeping in my marital trap."

Nita tipped the phone toward him. "I lost a sublet to a continuity-assured applicant. Which sounds like the sort of phrase that should make a person throw up."

He read the line, exhaled through his nose, and sat down across from her. "Did you at least get rejected before paying?"

"No. They charged me for the privilege of not existing in the correct configuration."

"Strong service culture."

She should not have appreciated that. The small flick of it in her chest annoyed her more than the app had. She took a sip of water instead and tasted copper from the pipes.

From the hallway came a faint floorboard complaint. Nita turned too late.

Leela stood there in her sleep T-shirt with one sock on and one off, blue glasses slightly crooked, her hair mashed flat on one side from the pillow. There was dried glue on the heel of her hand. She had gone quiet in the very particular way that meant she had been listening first and deciding second.

Her eyes went to the bags. One by the door would have been a fact. Two made a pattern.

"Are those both sleep bags?" she asked.

Ben straightened. "You're supposed to be asleep."

"That isn't an answer."

Nita put both feet on the floor. "Leela, come here."

Leela did not move closer. She stayed in the hallway where she could see the bags, both parents, and the apartment door at once.

"If you both have sleep bags," she said, "who gets me on Thursdays."

That was the question. It was not "Do you still love me," and it was not "Are you getting divorced," and it was not the mercy of a question adults already had language rehearsed around. It was Thursdays, the long day at school when pickup ran later and the badge shifted color at four-thirty because Nita's Claude schedule changed and Ben usually took the first handoff. It was a route question, an object question, a question with a deadline built into it.

Ben glanced at Nita, already looking for a softer version of the truth to wrap around the sharp part. Nita hated him for that reflex and loved him for having it with their daughter. The two feelings sat on top of each other so often now they had become one heavy thing.

"We're still figuring that out," Ben said.

Leela's expression did not change. "Thursday doesn't move."

"I know," Nita said.

Leela lifted her bare foot and set it down on the cooler tile, absorbing the room through her body the way she always did when upset. "If there's two houses, the Thursday backpack can't be wrong. The purple folder has to come back signed Friday. And my badge turns blue first and then green."

Neither of them answered.

The apartment hummed around them with its upgraded filters and smug little efficiencies. Somewhere in the fridge, ice shifted. The under-cabinet light sensed motion and brightened half a shade, as if illumination could count as help.

Leela looked from one adult to the other. Her voice got smaller, which was worse than crying.

"Do I need two toothbrushes?"

Ben opened his mouth. Closed it.

Nita saw, all at once, how little they had actually built for the child they kept telling themselves they were protecting: a paper packet, a lot of rage, some browser tabs, two overnight bags, and no answer for Thursday. She had spent the entire train ride home thinking about money and square footage and transit. Leela had gone directly to the only real question.

"Not yet," Nita said, because it was the only thing in the room she could make true. "You don't need two toothbrushes yet."

Leela kept watching her. "So which person is Thursday?"

Ben leaned forward, forearms on his knees. "Hey. For now, we're both taking you. When Thursday comes, we're both doing pickup. Nobody's disappearing on Thursday."

It was not a plan. It was triage dressed as certainty. Leela knew the difference. She had been living inside their version of it for weeks.

"Okay," she said.

She did not sound okay. She sounded like she had stored the answer for later use.

"Come here," Ben said again.

This time she came. He pulled her in against his side. Nita saw her daughter's face over Ben's shoulder, eyes open, one lens catching the kitchen light. Nita stood and went to them because staying seated would have turned the moment into observation, and observation had already done enough damage in this family.

Leela smelled like toothpaste and glue stick and the sweat from a child who slept hot. Nita touched the back of her neck. It was damp under the hairline.

"We're working on it," she said into the top of Leela's head, and hated how weak that sounded.

"You should work faster," Leela said.

Then she pulled away, picked up the loose sock from the hall runner, and went back to bed with the grave administrative disappointment of someone who had discovered the adults were behind schedule on her life.

Ben watched her door close. "Well."

"Don't say at least she's communicating."

"I wasn't going to."

"You were about to."

"I was going to say well."

"That's worse."

He sat back and rubbed both hands over his face. When he dropped them, he looked older in a way that had nothing to do with his hair. "The elevator wouldn't even let me fail privately."

Nita picked up her phone and turned the screen off. The black reflected both bags by the door.

"Neither would the listing."

They sat there listening to the apartment work.

***

After Leela finally went down for real, they spread the numbers out across the kitchen table like evidence from a case nobody would prosecute.

Nita had the Ridgewood screenshot with the rent jump and the ninety-five-dollar screening fee. Ben had the dead guest token from Pilar's building, the outbound ride estimate, the free return credit, and a hotel listing he had not booked because the nightly rate was three hundred and twelve dollars before tax and shame. Between them sat the co-op payment schedule, the special assessment reminder she had printed and then regretted printing, and the school custody-change page loading in circles on the old tablet because the newer screen belonged to work now more than home.

The kitchen was narrow enough that when Ben shifted on the bench his knee knocked the trash pullout. The apartment had recently decided their lighting should support circadian repair, which meant the overhead stayed dim while the under-cabinet strips shone directly on every bill and receipt. The whole table looked staged for a tasteful documentary about collapse.

Ben read from the tablet. "Alternate weekly pickup origin requires thirty-day review, proof of residence for each guardian, and a dual-route processing fee."

"How much?"

"Two hundred forty a month."

Nita held out her hand. He passed the tablet over. There it was in school-issued calm.

`Dual-route dependent support package: $240 monthly. Manual handoff delays may occur during review.`

"Support package," she said. "Of course."

"Maybe that's the discounted number."

"That was not a useful sentence."

He leaned back. "I'm trying to pace the despair."

"We don't need paced despair. We need arithmetic."

She pulled the legal pad from the drawer by the stove, then stopped with it half out. The pages were covered in Leela's marker tests and grocery math. She put it back and used the back of a takeout menu instead.

She wrote down the rent, the assessment, the co-op payment, the school fee, transit without bundled credits, extra groceries because one household separating into two did not get to eat one household's volume at one household's price, laundry doubled, and toiletries doubled. It was the ordinary stupid duplication of human life, which had once been the basic premise of divorce and now apparently required a punitive tariff.

Ben watched her write. "If I stay with Pilar three nights a week and I pay normal transit both ways, that's what, maybe one eighty extra."

"If she can even register you three nights a week."

"Right."

"And if I took Ridgewood at the adjusted rent, first month and deposit would be forty-four eighty before electric."

"Plus the fee."

"Thank you, yes, plus the fee. I did notice being robbed."

He put his palms up. "Noted."

She wrote the number anyway. The pen tore slightly through the glossy paper at the second four. It felt satisfying in a childish way.

Outside, a train screamed through the curve and the window in the kitchen bench shivered in its frame. The building always shook a little when the larger trains passed. Usually the vibration disappeared into the rest of household life. Tonight it moved through the tableware, the papers, Ben's water glass, the bones in Nita's wrist. Even the room was tired of holding them.

"What if we just do cash everything for a while," Ben said. "Old-school. No route stack, no bundled rides, no shared perks. I can walk more."

"And Leela."

"I know."

"And work." She did not raise her voice. She never needed to when precision would do more damage. "And the mortgage. And the assessment. And the fact that your employer is already tracking overnight location variance because otherwise you would be on Pilar's sofa right now pretending this was a transition instead of a failed errand."

Ben took that, then surprised her by not smoothing it away.

"Yeah," he said. "I know."

The admission landed harder than if he had argued.

He reached for his phone, unlocked it, and turned the screen toward her. A Gemini Civic summary card sat there in cheerful blue.

`Return-home continuity credit applied. Tomorrow's commute remains on bundle.`

"It comped the ride back," he said. "Not there. Back."

Nita looked at it until the words blurred. Something in her chest gave way, not toward tears, not toward rage, but toward a colder comprehension. The systems did not merely prefer the marriage. They had started billing against alternatives in real time. The punishment was granular, a fee here, a denied elevator there, a child support package recoded as user convenience. You could spend a whole life inside increments like that and never once get the dignity of a clean no.

"My listing repriced the second I marked Leela's route under review," she said.

Ben let out a tired breath. "Jesus."

"Not Jesus. Design."

He nodded once.

On the table her arithmetic had turned ugly fast. Even without hotel nights, even without duplicate furniture, and even before lawyers or filing fees or the simple cash hemorrhage of buying a second set of the things a child forgot to carry back and forth, they were already underwater. They could maybe afford separation in the technical sense if they stopped being the family they had spent fifteen years accumulating, sold the co-op, pulled Leela from school, commuted farther, gave up the neighborhood, and told their jobs to go to hell while accepting that hell might answer with unemployment.

That would come later if it came at all. Tonight they could not afford one successful night apart.

Ben saw the same sentence forming. "We're being fined for trying."

"Yes."

"Is that the official term?"

"No. The official term is probably resilience support."

Despite everything, his mouth twitched. "That's disgusting."

"I know."
